<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-05671-t005/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="fn3"><p>NR: not reported; CF: cefotaxime; K = kanamycin; T: Timentin; Spc = spectinomycin; AS: acetosyringone; PGIP: polygalacturase inhibitory proteins; PMI: phosphomannose isomerase; Carb: Carbenicillin; Rif: rifampicin; His<sup>-</sup>: histidine heterotrophy; GUS: β-glucuronidase; MES: 2-N-morpholineethanesulfonic acid; PVP: polyvinylpyrrolidone; CmR: Chloramphenicol; <em>Cs</em>GRF3: <em>Cannabis sative</em> growth-regulating factor3; <em>Cs</em>GIF1: <em>Cannabis sative</em> GRF-interacting factor1.</p></div></div></section><section id="sec4dot1dot1-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.1.1. <em>Agrobacterium</em> Strains</h4>
<p><em>Agrobacterium</em> strain selection is one of the most important factors in gene transformation (<a href="#ijms-22-05671-t005" class="usa-link">Table 5</a>). The first study of successful gene transformation with more than 50% transformation frequency in fiber-type cannabis (hemp) was performed by MacKinnon et al. [<a href="#B171-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B171-ijms-22-05671">171</a>]. However, they did not report which strain of <em>Agrobacterium</em> was used. Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B172-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172-ijms-22-05671">172</a>] obtained an acceptable transformation efficiency (15.1 to 55.3%) by using <em>A. tumefaciens</em> EHA101. Wahby et al. [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>] used three <em>A. tumefaciens</em> strains including LBA4404, C58, and IVIA 251, as well as eight <em>A. rhizogenes</em> strains including 476, 477, 478, A424, AR10GUS, A4, AR10, and R1601 for establishing hairy root cultures in different genotypes of cannabis. According to their results, genotypes had different responses to <em>Agrobacterium</em> strains in such a way that transformation efficiency ranged between 43% for AR10GUS to 98% for R1601 in <em>A. rhizogenes</em> strains, and between 33.7% for IVIA251 and 63% for C58 for <em>A. tumefaciens</em> strains. Generally, Wahby et al. [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>] reported that the gene transformation frequency in cannabis is dependent not only on the strains of <em>Agrobacterium</em> but also on cannabis genotypes, consisting of their sensitivity to agro-infection and their potential to regenerate transgenic tissues.</p>
<p>Deguchi et al. [<a href="#B175-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175-ijms-22-05671">175</a>] compared transformation efficiency among several hemp genotypes in including Ferimon, Fedora 17, USO31, Felina 32, Santhica 27, Futura 75, CRS-1, and CFX-2 using different <em>A. tumefaciens</em> strains including LBA4404, GV3101, and EHA105 and found high transformation efficiency (&gt;50%) for some genotypes. Based on their results, the maximum GUS expression was observed in the CRS-1 genotype and <em>A. tumefaciens</em> GV3101 led to the highest transformation frequency. In another study, Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] investigated the potential of <em>A. tumefaciens</em> EHA105 in transforming different cannabis genotypes (Candida CD-1, Holy Grail x CD-1, Green Crack CBD, and Nightingale) and obtained a high transformation efficiency (45–70.6%). Different responses to <em>Agrobacterium</em> strains are not unique to cannabis and it has been previously documented that various <em>Agrobacterium</em> strains differ in their capacity to transform different recalcitrant plants such as maize [<a href="#B177-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B177-ijms-22-05671">177</a>]. Therefore, it is necessary to investigate more strains to obtain efficient strains for a high-frequency gene transformation protocol.</p></section><section id="sec4dot1dot2-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.1.2. Infection of Explant</h4>
<p>The physiological condition and source of explants play a pivotal role in <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation. Different explants such as shoot tip and hypocotyl have been employed for gene transformation in cannabis (<a href="#ijms-22-05671-t005" class="usa-link">Table 5</a>). Most studies used different parts of in vitro grown seedlings. MacKinnon et al. [<a href="#B171-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B171-ijms-22-05671">171</a>] succeeded in gene transformation using shoot tip explants that were selected from greenhouse-grown cannabis. Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B172-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172-ijms-22-05671">172</a>] used callus cells derived from stem and leaf segments of cannabis for <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation. In another study, Wahby et al. [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>] used different parts of 5-day-old in vitro grown seedling of hemp including hypocotyls, cotyledons, cotyledonary node, and primary leaves for gene transformation and reported that the best gene transformation results were obtained from hypocotyl segments. Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] also used cotyledons and true leaves of 4-day-old in vitro grown seedling of hemp for gene transformation. Deguchi et al. [<a href="#B175-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175-ijms-22-05671">175</a>] reported a successful gene transformation using male and female flowers, stem, leaf, and root tissues derived from 2-month-old in vitro grown seedling of hemp.</p>
<p>The co-cultivation period and concentration of <em>Agrobacterium</em> inoculum (optical density (OD)) have a significant impact on successful gene transformation. Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B172-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172-ijms-22-05671">172</a>] suggested three-day co-cultivation and OD<sub>600nm</sub> 1.6–1.8 for gene transformation of callus cells. In another study, different explants were co-cultured for two days [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>]. Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] suggested three days of co-cultivation and OD<sub>600nm</sub> 0.6 for gene transformation of different parts of in vitro grown seedling of hemp.</p>
<p><em>Agrobacterium</em> infection efficiency in cannabis can be increased by adding chemical compounds, such as sodium citrate, acetosyringone, and mannose, to the co-cultivation medium. Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B172-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172-ijms-22-05671">172</a>] reported increased <em>Agrobacterium</em> infection using 100 µM acetosyringone and 2% mannose for hemp gene transformation in the co-cultivation medium. Wahby et al. [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>] studied the effect of different concentrations of acetosyringone (20, 100, and 200 µM), sucrose (0.5 and 2%), sodium citrate (20 mM), and 2-N-morpholineethanesulfonic acid (MES) (30 mM) on the gene transformation of cannabis and reported that different chemical compounds had little impact on <em>Agrobacterium</em> infection efficiency, and 20 µM acetosyringone resulted in the best results. On the other hand, Deguchi et al. [<a href="#B175-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175-ijms-22-05671">175</a>] applied 200 μM acetosyringone, 2% glucose, and 10 mM MES for increasing strain virulence in the gene transformation of hemp. Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] also reported increasing the <em>Agrobacterium</em> infectability by using 100 µM acetosyringone for cannabis gene transformation in the co-cultivation medium. Recently, Karthik et al. [<a href="#B178-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B178-ijms-22-05671">178</a>] reported that SNP improved the efficiency of <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation in soybean. Therefore, it is reasonable to investigate the effects of SNP on the gene transformation of cannabis. </p></section><section id="sec4dot1dot3-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.1.3. Selection Markers</h4>
<p>Although kanamycin has been the main selection agent of transgenic cannabis cells and tissues, other antibiotics, such as spectinomycin, rifampicin, and chloramphenicol, have also been successfully applied for selecting the transformed cells and tissues of cannabis [<a href="#B10-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-ijms-22-05671">10</a>,<a href="#B47-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47-ijms-22-05671">47</a>]. However, it is necessary to study the effect of other antibiotics on cannabis gene transformation because the response of various tissues and genotypes to different antibiotics may vary. For instance, Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] and Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B174-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B174-ijms-22-05671">174</a>] used spectinomycin- and kanamycin-resistant genes as selectable markers in the <em>Agrobacterium</em> vectors. Wahby et al. [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>] used <em>Agrobacterium</em> vectors carrying kanamycin-, carbenicillin-, and rifampicin-resistant genes for transforming cannabis. Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] also used kanamycin- and rifampicin-resistant genes in <em>Agrobacterium</em> vectors. Moreover, Deguchi et al. [<a href="#B175-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175-ijms-22-05671">175</a>] considered the chloramphenicol-resistant gene as a selective marker in <em>Agrobacterium</em> vectors. </p>
<p>Twin T-DNA binary vectors have also been successfully used for generating marker-free transgenic plants. This would be a very useful and promising method for generating marker-free transgenic cannabis and to mitigate scientific and public concerns regarding dispersing herbicide- and antibiotic-resistant genes of GMO products into the environment.</p></section><section id="sec4dot1dot4-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.1.4. Eliminating Chimerism</h4>
<p>The regeneration of chimeric tissue with both non-transformed and transformed cells and tissues is one of the most crucial challenges in developing a stable gene transformation system in different plants [<a href="#B179-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B179-ijms-22-05671">179</a>]. Therefore, it is essential to use an approach to eliminate the chimeric cells and regenerate only transgenic cells. Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B172-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B172-ijms-22-05671">172</a>] studied the gene transformation frequency and chimerism using the phosphomannose isomerase (PMI) selection strategy, which is based on the existence of sugar (mannose) in the medium. They compared two transformation procedures including 1% mannose and 300 mg/L Timentin (treatment 1) and 2% mannose and 150 mg/L Timentin (treatment 2) and reported that treatment 1 was not capable of distinguishing non-transgenic cells from transgenic cells, and, therefore, they suggested treatment 2 for gene transformation in cannabis. Wahby et al. [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>] compared the transformation performance and chimerism of two procedures of gene transformation: complex media MI1 (100 µM acetosyringone, 0.5% sucrose, and 30 mM MES) and MI2 (200 µM acetosyringone, 2% sucrose, 20 mM sodium citrate) and reported that these media could not completely detect transgenic tissues from non-transgenic tissues. Chimerism in transformation systems is not unique to cannabis and is a challenge in many species and is highly dependent on the regeneration system. Moving forward, developing an efficient somatic embryogenesis-based regeneration system will be important to mitigate this issue.</p></section><section id="sec4dot1dot5-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.1.5. Promoters and Translational Enhancer</h4>
<p>Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] and Feeney and Punja [<a href="#B174-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B174-ijms-22-05671">174</a>] used the binary vector pNOV3635 and pCAMBIA1301, respectively, carrying a coding region for PMI under control of the nopaline synthase terminator (NOS) and the ubiquitin promoter derived from <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> (Ubq3), as well as a spectinomycin and kanamycin selectable marker genes. They also used chlorophenol-red PMI assay, PCR, and southern blot for confirming gene transformation.</p>
<p>When the β-glucuronidase (GUS) reporter gene was employed using the cauliflower mosaic virus 35S RNA (CaMV35S) promoter, GUS activities were observed [<a href="#B173-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B173-ijms-22-05671">173</a>]. In another study, Deguchi et al. [<a href="#B175-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B175-ijms-22-05671">175</a>] used a pEarleyGate 101 vector harboring the eGFP gene and uidA gene under the control of the CaMV 35 S promoter and OCS terminator for GFP fluorescence and GUS staining assays. Moreover, Sorokin et al. [<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>] reported that when the GUS gene in the pCAMBIA1301 vector was under the control of a CaMV 35S:: Intron, the highest GUS activity in the transgenic cannabis was achieved. While future studies will undoubtedly establish more efficient, or tissue/age-specific promotors, existing promoters are generally effective in cannabis.</p></section></section><section id="sec4dot2-ijms-22-05671"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.2. Strategies to Improve Gene Transformation Efficiency</h3>
<p>Despite advances in the gene transformation of cannabis over the past few years, efficient transgenic regeneration remains an obstacle. The ability to express and introduce transgene and to regenerate de novo shoots or embryos are two important obstacles to producing transgenic cannabis. Recent studies have confirmed that cannabis cells can be efficiently transformed [<a href="#B10-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-ijms-22-05671">10</a>,<a href="#B47-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B47-ijms-22-05671">47</a>,<a href="#B176-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B176-ijms-22-05671">176</a>]; however, there is only one report of transgenic cannabis regeneration [<a href="#B93-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93-ijms-22-05671">93</a>]. The application of genes related to regulating plant growth and development such as WUSCHEL (WUS) [<a href="#B180-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-ijms-22-05671">180</a>], BABY BOOM (BBM) [<a href="#B181-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B181-ijms-22-05671">181</a>], and <em>Growth-Regulating Factors</em> (GRFs) alone or in combination with <em>GRF-Interacting Factor</em> (GIF) [<a href="#B182-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-ijms-22-05671">182</a>] is reported as a promising approach to increase plant regeneration efficiency [<a href="#B170-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B170-ijms-22-05671">170</a>]. In this way, ectopic overexpression of genes involved in meristem maintenance, somatic embryogenesis, or phytohormone metabolism can be used to overcome plant regeneration obstacles in recalcitrant plants [<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>]. Numerous genes involved in meristem maintenance, somatic embryogenesis, and phytohormone metabolism have been identified in different plants [<a href="#B180-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-ijms-22-05671">180</a>,<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>,<a href="#B184-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B184-ijms-22-05671">184</a>]. Accordingly, these studies led to novel approaches for in vitro plant regeneration and <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation research on recalcitrant species. The downside of this approach is that morphogenic genes have adverse pleiotropic impacts and should be removed from transformed or edited plants [<a href="#B180-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-ijms-22-05671">180</a>,<a href="#B182-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-ijms-22-05671">182</a>,<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>,<a href="#B184-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B184-ijms-22-05671">184</a>]. The next sections presented recent progress in using morphogenic genes and highlighted possible approaches to overcome negative pleiotropic effects.</p>
<section id="sec4dot2dot1-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.2.1. Morphogenic Genes, Key Factors in Plant Regeneration</h4>
<p>Morphogenic genes that induce regeneration, when overexpressed, are categorized into two classes according to their growth reactions. The first group is composed of genes (e.g., SERK1, AGL15, WUS, and STM) that improve a pre-existing response of embryogenesis under in vitro conditions [<a href="#B180-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-ijms-22-05671">180</a>]. The second group includes genes (e.g., BBM, EMK, RKD4, LEC1, LEC2, L1L, HAP3A, FUS3, WUS, WOX5, KN1, CUC1, CUC2, ESR1, and ESR2) that stimulate ectopic embryogenesis or meristem induction under in vitro conditions where such events are usually not seen [<a href="#B180-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-ijms-22-05671">180</a>,<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>,<a href="#B185-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185-ijms-22-05671">185</a>,<a href="#B186-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B186-ijms-22-05671">186</a>]. The roles of morphogenic genes in plant regeneration have been summarized in <a href="#ijms-22-05671-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-05671-f007"><h5 class="obj_head">Figure 7.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8197860_ijms-22-05671-g007.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/9838/8197860/c156dc97d747/ijms-22-05671-g007.jpg" loading="lazy" height="326" width="760" alt="Figure 7"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/ijms-22-05671-f007/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>A schematic view of the morphogenic genes demonstrating their roles in plant growth and development as well as in vitro plant regeneration (AGL15: AGAMOUS-LIKE15; ARR: ARABIDOPSIS RESPONSE REGULATOR; BBM: BABY BOOM; CLV3: CLAVATA3; CUC: CUP-SHAPED COTYLEDON; ESR: ENHANCER OF SHOOT REGENERATION; FUS3: FUSCA3; GA3ox: Gibberellin 3-beta-dioxygenase; IAA30: Indole acetic acid inducible 30; LEC: LEAFY COTYLEDON; PIN1: PIN-FORMED 1; PKL: PICKLE; PLT: PLETHORA; PRC: Polycomb repressive complex; STM: SHOOT MERISTEMLESS; TAA: TRYPTOPHAN AMINOTRANSFERASE ARABIDOPSIS; WOX: WUSCHEL-related homeobox; WUS: WUSCHEL; YUC: YUCCA).</p></figcaption></figure><p>In the first class, the overexpression of genes leads to an increase in embryogenesis under in vitro conditions in which the formation of somatic embryos is already observed. For instance, overexpression of <em>SOMATIC EMBRYOGENESIS RECEPTOR KINASE1</em> (SERK1) using CaMV 35S promoter resulted in ~4-fold and 2-fold increases in somatic embryogenesis of <em>Arabidopsis thaliana</em> [<a href="#B187-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B187-ijms-22-05671">187</a>] and <em>Coffea canephora</em> [<a href="#B188-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B188-ijms-22-05671">188</a>], respectively. Similarly, further studies in Arabidopsis [<a href="#B189-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B189-ijms-22-05671">189</a>], cotton [<a href="#B190-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B190-ijms-22-05671">190</a>], and soybean [<a href="#B191-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B191-ijms-22-05671">191</a>] have shown an increase in somatic embryogenesis with overexpression of the <em>AGAMOUS-LIKE15</em> (AGL15) gene, a member of the SERK1 protein complex [<a href="#B192-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B192-ijms-22-05671">192</a>]. It is also well documented that SERK1 and SERK3 are the co-receptors of the <em>Brassinosteroid Insensitive 1</em> (BRI1) protein. <em>Brassinosteroid</em> is a known PGR that plays a key role in plant embryogenesis. The members of SERK proteins seem to act as mediators across the plasma membrane for brassinosteroid signaling [<a href="#B193-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B193-ijms-22-05671">193</a>].</p>
<p>The use of meristem formation-related genes such as <em>SHOOT MERISTEMLESS</em> (STM) [<a href="#B194-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B194-ijms-22-05671">194</a>] or <em>A. thaliana</em> WUSCHEL (<em>At</em>WUS), a key gene for regulating meristem cell fate [<a href="#B195-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B195-ijms-22-05671">195</a>], have also resulted in improving embryogenic responses. Arroyo-Herrera et al. [<a href="#B196-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B196-ijms-22-05671">196</a>] reported that somatic embryogenesis in <em>Coffea canephora</em> was increased up to 3–5 fold using an estradiol-inducible <em>At</em>WUS construct. In line with this result, Bouchabké-Coussa et al. [<a href="#B197-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B197-ijms-22-05671">197</a>] reported that using the 35S::<em>At</em>WUS cassette led to a 3-fold increase in somatic embryogenesis of <em>Gossypium hirsutum</em> after <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation. In another study, microspore-derived embryogenesis of <em>Brassica napus</em> was increased using the 35S::<em>Bn</em>STM construct [<a href="#B198-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B198-ijms-22-05671">198</a>].</p>
<p>In the second class, gene overexpression leads to spontaneous regeneration or direct ectopic regeneration of meristems or embryo-like structures in the lack of inductive in vitro conditions. The early studies have shown that overexpression of the <em>At</em>WUS gene [<a href="#B199-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B199-ijms-22-05671">199</a>] and <em>B. napus</em> BABY BOOM (<em>Bn</em>BBM) gene, a member of the AP2/ERF transcription factors (TFs) [<a href="#B200-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B200-ijms-22-05671">200</a>], leads to embryonic morphogenesis. Consequently, Boutilier et al. [<a href="#B200-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B200-ijms-22-05671">200</a>] reported that the constitutive expression of the <em>Bn</em>BBM gene in <em>A. thaliana</em> led to ectopic somatic embryogenesis and these ectopic embryos could generate plantlets in the lack of PGRs. This approach has been successfully used to regenerate the transgenic T<sub>0</sub> plantlets in different plants using orthologs of the <em>Bn</em>BBM gene such as transforming 35S::<em>At</em>BBM into <em>Nicotiana tabacum</em> [<a href="#B201-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B201-ijms-22-05671">201</a>], soybean 35S::<em>Gm</em>BBM into <em>A. thaliana</em> [<a href="#B202-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B202-ijms-22-05671">202</a>], oil palm 35S::<em>Eg</em>BBM into <em>A. thaliana</em> [<a href="#B203-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B203-ijms-22-05671">203</a>], and 35S::<em>Tc</em>BBM into <em>Theobroma cacao</em> [<a href="#B204-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B204-ijms-22-05671">204</a>]. In another study, Tsuwamoto et al. [<a href="#B205-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B205-ijms-22-05671">205</a>] reported that ectopic overexpression of <em>A. thaliana EMBRYOMAKER</em> (<em>At</em>EMK) led to embryo-like structures at 23% of cotyledon tips; however, these structures could not regenerate the plantlets. They concluded that <em>At</em>EMK, a member of the AP2/ERF TFs and related to BBM, should be expressed under a regulated network for avoiding pleiotropic effects and achieving normal plantlets. RKD4 as a key gene in the RWP-RK TF family is another gene that has an important role during early embryo development in <em>A. thaliana</em> and plays a pivotal role in the first asymmetrical zygotic division [<a href="#B206-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B206-ijms-22-05671">206</a>,<a href="#B207-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B207-ijms-22-05671">207</a>]. For instance, ectopic somatic embryogenesis of <em>Phalaenopsis</em>, a plant typically reluctant to direct somatic embryogenesis, was obtained using the chemical induction of transgenic RKD4 [<a href="#B208-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B208-ijms-22-05671">208</a>].</p>
<p>The overexpression of genes related to embryo maturation (e.g., <em>FUSCA3</em> (FUS3), <em>LEAFY COTYLEDON1</em> (LEC1), and LEC2) causes similar morphogenic reactions. Lotan et al. [<a href="#B209-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B209-ijms-22-05671">209</a>] characterized the first of these genes namely LEC1, in which a cassette of 35S::<em>At</em>LEC1 was transformed to <em>A. thaliana</em>. Although the embryo-like structures were produced, functional ectopic embryogenesis was not observed [<a href="#B209-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B209-ijms-22-05671">209</a>]. In another study, Zhu et al. [<a href="#B210-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B210-ijms-22-05671">210</a>] used a 35S::CsL1L cassette for overexpression of <em>Citrus sinensis</em> LEC1 after gene transformation in epicotyls of sweet orange and reported that embryo-like structures were obtained after two months. They concluded that overexpression of L1L in Citrus is sufficient for recovering functional somatic embryos. However, Uddenberg et al. [<a href="#B211-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B211-ijms-22-05671">211</a>] indicated that although the overexpression of the LEC1/L1L (PaHAP3A) did not lead to ectopic embryogenesis in vegetative tissues, ectopic embryogenesis was obtained during zygotic embryo maturation. These findings showed that certain types of cells and tissues may be more receptive to morphogenic genes for enhancing ectopic embryogenesis or meristem maintenance [<a href="#B211-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B211-ijms-22-05671">211</a>]. Later studies have shown that the overexpression of LEC2 leads to more somatic embryogenesis in comparison with LEC1, FUS3, or L1L [<a href="#B212-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B212-ijms-22-05671">212</a>,<a href="#B213-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B213-ijms-22-05671">213</a>,<a href="#B214-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B214-ijms-22-05671">214</a>].</p>
<p>However, several studies [<a href="#B190-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B190-ijms-22-05671">190</a>,<a href="#B197-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B197-ijms-22-05671">197</a>,<a href="#B198-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B198-ijms-22-05671">198</a>,<a href="#B200-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B200-ijms-22-05671">200</a>] showed that pre-existing embryogenic responses are increased through overexpression of morphogenic genes, and additional studies revealed that ectopic overexpression of morphogenic genes resulted in improving somatic embryogenesis where such events are usually not seen [<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>]. Zuo et al. [<a href="#B199-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B199-ijms-22-05671">199</a>] identified the first “meristem” gene in Arabidopsis (<em>At</em>WUS) which enhances somatic embryogenesis. They suggested that the vegetative-to-embryonic transition can be stimulated by the WUS gene. Moreover, Gallois et al. [<a href="#B215-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B215-ijms-22-05671">215</a>] demonstrated that the overexpression of WUS and STM genes generates the bulk of cells contiguous to the WUS foci showing primary meristems. In another study, Gallois et al. [<a href="#B216-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B216-ijms-22-05671">216</a>] indicated that unique phenotypes obtained from WUS overexpression were related to different factors such as co-expression of other morphogenic TFs, phytohormone regime, and type of WUS activation (such as GAL4-VP16 activation method and HSP::CRE-mediated excision). Rashid et al. [<a href="#B217-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B217-ijms-22-05671">217</a>] also reported that a member of the WUS/WOX gene family namely <em>At</em>WOX5 led to the direct organogenic response in <em>Nicotiana tabacum</em>. Luo et al. [<a href="#B218-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B218-ijms-22-05671">218</a>] showed that shoot organogenesis in <em>N. tabacum</em> was increased 3-fold by overexpression of the maize STM ortholog <em>KNOTTED1</em> (KN1) through 35S::<em>Zm</em>KN1 cassette. Similar results were reported by Nishimura et al. [<a href="#B219-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B219-ijms-22-05671">219</a>].</p>
<p>The <em>CUP-SHAPED COTYLEDON1</em> (CUC1) and CUC2 genes are known to have a key role in the shoot meristem formation. Daimon et al. [<a href="#B220-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B220-ijms-22-05671">220</a>] indicated that the overexpression of <em>At</em>CUC1 and <em>At</em>CUC2 under a CaMV 35S promoter resulted in a significant increase (about 6.5–9-fold) in shoot organogenesis of <em>A. thaliana</em> transgenic calli. However, no micro-shoots were recovered in the lack of hormones, showing the hormone-dependent function of CUC1 and CUC2 genes. Genes related to the phytohormone signal transduction (both downstream targets and receptors), play a pivotal role in improving plant regeneration; genes like <em>ENHANCER OF SHOOT REGENERATION 1</em> (ESR1) and ESR2 that are involved in the cytokinin response pathway and <em>MONOPTEROS</em> (MP) that is involved in the auxin response pathway. Several studies have shown that the overexpression of these genes led to an increase in the shoot meristem formation [<a href="#B221-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B221-ijms-22-05671">221</a>,<a href="#B222-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B222-ijms-22-05671">222</a>,<a href="#B223-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B223-ijms-22-05671">223</a>]. Moreover, the phenotypic response of morphogenic genes can be impacted by levels of phytohormones. For instance, Wójcikowska et al. [<a href="#B224-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B224-ijms-22-05671">224</a>] reported that the high and low concentrations of auxin led to callogenesis and somatic embryogenesis, respectively, in the transformed <em>A. thaliana</em> overexpressing LEC2 under a dexamethasone (DEX)-inducible system.</p></section><section id="sec4dot2dot2-ijms-22-05671"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">4.2.2. Strategies to Overcome Pleiotropic Effects</h4>
<p>Based on the aforementioned literature and other references therein, it is clear that the use of morphogenic genes may help to increase regeneration efficiency in plants. However, strong and constitutive overexpression of these genes can lead to unwanted pleiotropic effects such as infertility of regenerated plants [<a href="#B170-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B170-ijms-22-05671">170</a>]. A promising approach to mitigate this is to implement an inducible expression system for these genes in a stable transformation system. This means, it is necessary to apply an additional step that includes optimization of the morphogenic gene expression level with restricting expression after transformation. Such an approach causes enhanced gene transformation and improved fertile and healthy T<sub>0</sub> plantlets regeneration. Generally, there are five strategies to cope with these challenges including (i) removing the morphogenic gene when no longer needed, (ii) using the inducible expression of the gene for improving morphogenic growth response followed by excision of the inducing ligand for silencing the expression, (iii) using <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation in such a way that promotes the transient expression of the morphogenic genes, (iv) using promoter that can turn off the morphogenic regulatory gene when no longer required, and (v) using GRF-GIF Chimeras [<a href="#B180-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B180-ijms-22-05671">180</a>,<a href="#B182-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-ijms-22-05671">182</a>,<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>,<a href="#B185-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B185-ijms-22-05671">185</a>,<a href="#B186-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B186-ijms-22-05671">186</a>]. </p>
<p>The excision-based method has provided a reliable strategy for applying morphogenic genes to regenerate fertile and healthy transgenic plantlets. The first successful application of this method through BBM and <em>Flippase</em> (FLP)-<em>recombinase</em> was reported in <em>Populus tomentosa</em> [<a href="#B225-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B225-ijms-22-05671">225</a>]. A T-DNA construct including a single pair of <em>FLP Recombination Target Sites</em> (FRT) encompassing both a CaMV 35S promoter stimulating <em>B. campestris</em> BBM (<em>Bc</em>BBM) expression and a heat-shock inducible promoter stimulating expression of FLP recombinase was designed. Deng et al. [<a href="#B225-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B225-ijms-22-05671">225</a>] reported that after transforming this T-DNA construct, more than 28% of calli produced normal plantlets on phytohormone-free media and also showed that 42 °C heat shock treatment for 2 h resulted in the removal of both the BBM and FLP recombinase cassettes. In another study, Lowe et al. [<a href="#B226-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B226-ijms-22-05671">226</a>] reported that high transformation efficiency in inbred maize was obtained by low expression of <em>Zm</em>WUS2 (using the <em>Agrobacterium NOPALINE SYNTHASE</em>, or NOS, promoter) and overexpression of <em>Zm</em>BBM (using the <em>Zea mays UBIQUITIN</em> promoter). The explants were inoculated on dry filter paper to induce a desiccation-stimulated maize promoter derived from an ABA-responsive gene (RAB17) carrying CRE recombinase, which then removed these 3 expression cassettes. After the removal of the BBM, WUS2, and CRE transgenes, only the T-DNA construct with the genes of interest were remained. This method has been successfully used in other recalcitrant plants such as sorghum, rice, corn, and sugarcane [<a href="#B227-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B227-ijms-22-05671">227</a>].</p>
<p>Inducible expression to control morphogenic gene expression is another robust alternative approach. Heidmann et al. [<a href="#B228-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B228-ijms-22-05671">228</a>] reported that gene transformation in <em>Capsicum annum</em>, a recalcitrant plant species, was achieved using the 35S::<em>Bn</em>BBM~GR vector for gene transformation and cultivation of explants in the medium consisting of DEX and TDZ. In a similar study, Lutz et al. [<a href="#B229-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B229-ijms-22-05671">229</a>] reported that transgenic fertile and healthy <em>A. thaliana</em> plantlets were achieved using the DEX-inducible <em>At</em>BBM~GR cassette.</p>
<p>The overexpression of <em>PLANT GROWTH ACTIVATION</em> genes, such as PGA37 in <em>A. thaliana</em> led to the transition of the vegetative phase to the embryogenic phase by using the estradiol-inducible system [<a href="#B230-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B230-ijms-22-05671">230</a>]. The PGA37 gene, based on the DNA-binding domain similarities, encodes the MYB118. Wang et al. [<a href="#B230-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B230-ijms-22-05671">230</a>] reported that root segments developed somatic embryos by PGA37 expression under inducible control, which were related to overexpression of LEC1. The green-yellowish embryonic calli were produced through the expression of PGA37 using the estradiol-inducible system in the medium containing auxin after 7–10 days of inoculation and somatic embryos were obtained after 3–5 weeks. When estradiol was removed from the medium, which causes downregulating PGA37 expression, healthy, fertile plantlets were obtained from the somatic embryos. Moreover, Wang et al. [<a href="#B230-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B230-ijms-22-05671">230</a>] showed that estradiol-induced expression of MYB115, a closely related homolog, resulted in somatic embryogenesis from root segments. A similar strategy using DEX-induced expression of <em>Tc</em>LEC2 was successfully used for the regeneration of transgenic somatic embryos in <em>Theobroma cacao</em> [<a href="#B231-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B231-ijms-22-05671">231</a>].</p>
<p>Using <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation in such a way that promotes the transient expression of the morphogenic genes is another alternative strategy [<a href="#B232-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B232-ijms-22-05671">232</a>,<a href="#B233-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B233-ijms-22-05671">233</a>,<a href="#B234-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B234-ijms-22-05671">234</a>,<a href="#B235-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B235-ijms-22-05671">235</a>]. Negative selectable markers located outside the T-DNA (beyond the left border) have been used to remove plant cells containing these sequences [<a href="#B233-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B233-ijms-22-05671">233</a>]. Other studies have located a positive marker gene outside the T-DNA, which could be transiently expressed for generating marker-free transgenic plants [<a href="#B236-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B236-ijms-22-05671">236</a>]. A mixture of T-DNAs could be received by placing an <em>Agrobacterium</em>-derived isopentyl transferase (IPT) gene outside the T-DNA, with the greater part of the T-strands containing the trait and a minority of T-strands not terminated properly outside the T-DNA, including the flanking IPT gene. Therefore, the transient expression of the IPT gene could stimulate the signaling of cytokinin and subsequently improve shoot proliferation, which results in the recovery of transgenic plants without a selectable marker. This strategy was successfully used in maize genotypes by positioning WUS2 and BBM beyond the left border for transient somatic embryogenesis and subsequent transgenic plantlet regeneration [<a href="#B183-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B183-ijms-22-05671">183</a>]. </p>
<p>Recently, a new strategy has been developed using the maize <em>phospholipid transfer protein</em> (PLTP) promoter driving BBM and the maize <em>auxin inducible</em> (AXIG1) promoter for improving gene transformation [<a href="#B237-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B237-ijms-22-05671">237</a>]. The application of these two promoters in the expression cassettes led to somatic embryogenesis within a week, and germination of these somatic embryos within 3–4 weeks. Similarly, the use of a combination of GRF and GIF has been proposed as a new method to tackle negative pleiotropic effects [<a href="#B170-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B170-ijms-22-05671">170</a>,<a href="#B182-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B182-ijms-22-05671">182</a>,<a href="#B238-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B238-ijms-22-05671">238</a>]. Debernardi et al. [<a href="#B170-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B170-ijms-22-05671">170</a>] demonstrated that fertile transgenic wheat, rice, and citrus without obvious developmental defects can be achieved by the expression of a fusion protein combining wheat GRF4 and GIF1. Moreover, they reported that GRF4–GIF1 induced efficient plant regeneration in the absence of exogenous PGRs which helps transgenic plant selection without selectable markers. They also combined CRISPR–Cas9 with GRF4– GIF1 and regenerated edited transgenic plants. Therefore, the combination of CRISPR–Cas9 and GRF4– GIF1 can be considered a powerful and promising strategy for the regeneration of healthy and fertile transgenic plants [<a href="#B170-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B170-ijms-22-05671">170</a>]. This strategy has been recently used in cannabis. Zhang et al. [<a href="#B93-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93-ijms-22-05671">93</a>] reported that using GRF3– GIF1 in the CRISPR vector resulted in a 1.7-fold increase in edited plant regeneration.</p>
<p>As a future perspective, morphogenic genes can be considered as targets of gene transformation in order to overcome current obstacles in cannabis tissue culture and successful regeneration of transformed plants. Furthermore, useful techniques enabled us to control the side effects of ectopic expression of these genes through transient and inducible gene expression in the host.</p></section></section><section id="sec4dot3-ijms-22-05671"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.3. Strategies to Prevent Transgene Escape</h3>
<p>The frequency of the alleles/genes in a population can be changed due to outcrossing of gametes or gene flow [<a href="#B239-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B239-ijms-22-05671">239</a>]. Thus, transgenes can move from a transgenic plant to their non-transgenic counterparts or wild relatives, a process called transgene escape. It is not uncommon for transgenic plants to mate with their wild relatives. Spontaneous hybridization will occur among transgenic and non-transgenic plants unless the proper distances are maintained [<a href="#B239-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B239-ijms-22-05671">239</a>]. This is an area much of concern, specifically in outcrossing plants, in plant biotechnology. Outcrossing poses negative impacts in terms of contamination in non-transgenic crops but this problem depends on whether a new allele causes an increase in transgene escape or not [<a href="#B240-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B240-ijms-22-05671">240</a>]. While dealing with trans-gene flow one should consider the situations according to transgenic crops [<a href="#B240-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B240-ijms-22-05671">240</a>]. In general, the possible containment and mitigation strategies are physical containment [<a href="#B241-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B241-ijms-22-05671">241</a>], biological/molecular containments (e.g., sterility [<a href="#B242-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B242-ijms-22-05671">242</a>], clistogamy [<a href="#B243-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B243-ijms-22-05671">243</a>], apomixes [<a href="#B244-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B244-ijms-22-05671">244</a>], maternal transformation [<a href="#B245-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B245-ijms-22-05671">245</a>], incompatible genome [<a href="#B246-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B246-ijms-22-05671">246</a>], gene splitting [<a href="#B247-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B247-ijms-22-05671">247</a>], expression in virus [<a href="#B248-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B248-ijms-22-05671">248</a>], genetic use restriction technology (GURT) [<a href="#B239-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B239-ijms-22-05671">239</a>]), and transgenic mitigation [<a href="#B249-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B249-ijms-22-05671">249</a>]. The appropriate approaches should be considered after transgenic cannabis production to prevent transgene escape.</p></section><section id="sec4dot4-ijms-22-05671"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">4.4. CRISPR/Cas-Mediated Genome Editing</h3>
<p>CRISPR/Cas-mediated genome editing has exceptionally improved plant biotechnology [<a href="#B43-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-ijms-22-05671">43</a>,<a href="#B250-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B250-ijms-22-05671">250</a>]. This system is robust and offers relatively high target programmability and specificity that can allow accurate genetic modification. The CRISPR/Cas system provides a unique opportunity to improve cannabis varieties with desired traits in a sustainable fashion. Recently, Zhang et al. [<a href="#B93-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93-ijms-22-05671">93</a>] employed the CRISPR/Cas9 system to knock out the phytoene desaturase gene and they reported that four edited cannabis plantlets with albino phenotype have been successfully generated. However, the numerous new biotechnological methods such as base editing and prime editing based on CRISPR/Cas platforms would expand cannabis synthetic biology and the toolbox of fundamental research. A successful CRISPR/Cas system experiment requires designing target-specific guide RNAs (gRNAs) and an efficient regeneration protocol for developing transgenic/edited plantlets [<a href="#B251-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B251-ijms-22-05671">251</a>]. The gRNA is categorized as a chimeric RNA including a CRISPR RNA (crRNA) and a trans-activating crRNA (tracrRNA). The crRNA consists of a guide (spacer) sequence that accurately navigates the Cas9 protein to the targeted gene. Then, the targeted DNA is cleaved by the RNA-guided DNA endonuclease Cas9. Another key part of CRISPR/Cas-mediated genome editing is the protospacer adjacent motif (PAM), which is a conserved and CRISPR-dependent DNA sequence motif adjacent to the target site (protospacer) and is utilized by the endogenous CRISPR in archaea and bacteria to discriminate invading- and self-DNAs. Generally, the application of an effective and precise CRISPR/Cas system is strongly dependent on the selection of the best guide sequence (gRNA target site) [<a href="#B43-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-ijms-22-05671">43</a>,<a href="#B250-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B250-ijms-22-05671">250</a>,<a href="#B251-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B251-ijms-22-05671">251</a>]. </p>
<p>In the previous sections, promising approaches for developing tissue culture protocols for genetic material delivery (e.g., <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated method) and regeneration methods have been discussed. In this section, we discuss the principles of designing gRNA in order to produce precise target mutation(s) and prevent off-target mutations as one of the most important prerequisites of genome editing technology. We also present the available bioinformatics tools for designing gRNAs that can be used in cannabis.</p>
<p>The prediction of the presence of off-target sites (i.e., unintended mutations) is one of the most important steps in designing gRNA for CRISPR/Cas-mediated genome editing. The design of the candidate gRNAs starts with the screening of the organism’s whole-genome sequence. Therefore, the availability and accessibility of a high-quality reference genome is required, which is the case in cannabis [<a href="#B252-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B252-ijms-22-05671">252</a>]. The genome of cannabis has been de novo assembled nearly a decade ago [<a href="#B253-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B253-ijms-22-05671">253</a>]; however, there are still some major challenges to using the cannabis reference genome. Currently, 12 different genomes (assembled and annotated) are available for cannabis [<a href="#B252-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B252-ijms-22-05671">252</a>]. Having multiple reference genomes can be considered a bonus, but, contradictorily, there are significant differences in reported genome size, chromosome order, and gene annotations among cannabis genome assemblies [<a href="#B1-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-ijms-22-05671">1</a>,<a href="#B2-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-ijms-22-05671">2</a>,<a href="#B252-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B252-ijms-22-05671">252</a>]. Therefore, designing a precise and accurate gRNA is an important starting point for high-quality CRISPR/Cas-mediated genome editing in cannabis with the minimum off-target activities. As can be seen in <a href="#ijms-22-05671-f008" class="usa-link">Figure 8</a>, there are three types of off-targets induced by the CRISPR/Cas system including (a) off-target sites with a base mismatch, (b) off-target sites with extra base (DNA bulge or deletion), and (c) off-target site with missing base (RNA bulge or insertion) [<a href="#B254-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B254-ijms-22-05671">254</a>]. Cases (b) and (c) are considered as the indel (insertion or deletion) off-target events [<a href="#B254-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B254-ijms-22-05671">254</a>]. It is necessary to consider these off-targets during genome engineering projects.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-05671-f008"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 8.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8197860_ijms-22-05671-g008.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/9838/8197860/6a842e39852c/ijms-22-05671-g008.jpg" loading="lazy" height="397" width="777" alt="Figure 8"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/ijms-22-05671-f008/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Three types of off-targets induced by CRISPR-mediated genome editing; (<strong>a</strong>) off-target sites with base mismatch, (<strong>b</strong>) off-target sites with extra base (DNA bulge or deletion), and (<strong>c</strong>) off-target site with missing base (RNA bulge or insertion).</p></figcaption></figure><p>Over the past few years, several bioinformatics tools have been developed to design gRNAs and predict, in silico<em>,</em> the off-targets. These tools have significantly facilitated the successful application of CRISPR/Cas-mediated genome editing technology [<a href="#B43-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B43-ijms-22-05671">43</a>,<a href="#B250-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B250-ijms-22-05671">250</a>]. A list of bioinformatics tools that contain cannabis genome information is presented in <a href="#ijms-22-05671-t006" class="usa-link">Table 6</a>. </p>

<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="table/ijms-22-05671-t006/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="fn4"><p>+ and - indicate the existence and lack of the feature, respectively.</p></div></div></section><p>Generally, these tools exploit a similar algorithm to design gRNA [<a href="#B255-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-ijms-22-05671">255</a>]. However, some characteristics related to the gRNA spacer region (e.g., number and type of mismatch, GC-content, length, and indels) and the selection of specific Cas-nucleases vary between these tools [<a href="#B256-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B256-ijms-22-05671">256</a>]. In general, these tools provide multiple sequences for designing gRNA, which are appropriate for editing similar genome conservative motives of various evolutionarily related genotypes [<a href="#B256-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B256-ijms-22-05671">256</a>]. These tools require the sequence of the targeted gene and the Cas-nucleases type. Then they search the genome and provide information related to candidate gRNAs such as the potential protospacers, both non-ranked and ranked with respect to their fitness, and off-target sites [<a href="#B255-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-ijms-22-05671">255</a>]. Generally, considering variations in the nucleotide sequences and the increase in individual gene copy number, it is necessary to design multiple gRNAs for each target for an efficient genome editing.</p>
<p>CRISPR RGEN is a web-based tool that also includes BE-Designer [<a href="#B257-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B257-ijms-22-05671">257</a>], Cas-OFFinder [<a href="#B258-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B258-ijms-22-05671">258</a>], Cas-Designer [<a href="#B259-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B259-ijms-22-05671">259</a>], Microhomology-Predictor [<a href="#B260-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B260-ijms-22-05671">260</a>], and some other tools that can be employed for post-editing analysis. Implementation of multiple tools increases the flexibility of CRISPR RGEN Tools that can be illustrated by the following examples: The Cas-Designer can detect potential protospacers in the analyzed sequence. It also provides a broad range of nucleases containing mutant forms with various PAM-sequences: SpCas9 (NRG for the off-target sites and NGG for the target sites), SaCas9 (NNGRRT), StCas9 (NNAGAAW), VRER SpCas9 (NGCG), wtSpCas9 (NNGTGA), NmCas9 (NNNNGMTT), VQR SpCas9 (NGA), CjCas9 (NNNNRYAC), CjCas9 (NNNVRYAC), BhCas12b (TTN), FnCpf1 (KYTV), FnCpf1 (TTN), AsCpf1 (TTTV), AsCpf1 (TTTN), etc. In the protospacer searches, one may specify if it is essential to find the probability of single nucleotide indels. In this case, the tool notifies about enhancing the time of the search. However, if this option is not applied, the results of the search returns immediately. Other parameters are chosen by default. Cannabis genome should be selected from the list to run the search. Further changes to other options of the Cas-Designer can be applied when it is locally installed on a computer [<a href="#B255-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-ijms-22-05671">255</a>]. The Microhomology-Predictor facilitates the forecast of the type of microhomology-mediated end-joining (MMEJ) and nonhomologous end-joining (NHEJ) by analyzing the microhomology of nucleotide sequences in the sites of double-strand breaks [<a href="#B260-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B260-ijms-22-05671">260</a>]. The Cas-OFFinder tool can be used to screen possible off-target sites in the genome. Furthermore, this tool can be independently applied for previously selected gRNAs to find off-target sites. For analysis, it is essential to insert the selected gRNAs sequences, specify the number of indels (both in RNA and DNA) and the adequate number of mismatched nucleotides, similarly Cas-Designer [<a href="#B258-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B258-ijms-22-05671">258</a>]. The BE-Designer tool can be applied for designing gRNAs to edit individual nitrogenous bases. Although Cas-Designer works similarly to BE-Designer, there are some differences. For instance, the selection of the corresponding nucleases and the PAM-regions is slightly limited in the BE-Designer, and the “window” size should be chosen for searching the targeted substitution of nucleotides (A→G or C→T) and the editing sites. The results would be provided in a table, in which the substituted nucleotides are shown with a specific color [<a href="#B255-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-ijms-22-05671">255</a>].</p>
<p>The CRISPOR [<a href="#B261-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B261-ijms-22-05671">261</a>] is another user-friendly tool with a detailed user manual. Selecting optimal protospacers in this tool can be performed in three steps. In the first step, the sequence of the targeted gene should be provided by the user. In the second step, the genome (e.g., cannabis) should be selected. In the third step, the Cas9 nuclease must be selected out of 33 options. The results of the search are provided in both table and graphic formats. The analyzed sequence is represented, and the potential edited sites are highlighted with various colors, relating to low, medium, and high specificity. Moreover, the table of the results includes information regarding the “Cloning/PCR primers”, protospacers sequences, their positions with indicated DNA chains, expression of a specific gRNA, and cloning. Two algorithms are used to assess the efficiency of the proposed gRNAs and, also, specificity is used to rank the proposed gRNAs. In addition to predicting the types of microhomology, it foresees the off-target sites with the number of mismatches [<a href="#B262-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B262-ijms-22-05671">262</a>].</p>
<p>The CRISPR/Cas9 Target online predictor (CCTop) tool [<a href="#B263-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B263-ijms-22-05671">263</a>] is applied to design and screen gRNAs for on-target and off-target sites. It can use a wide range of Cas-9 nucleases from various bacterial strains (e.g., <em>Campylobacter jejuni</em>, <em>Streptococcus pyogenes</em>, <em>Treponema denticola</em>, <em>Staphylococcus aureus</em>, <em>Streptococcus thermophilus</em>, and <em>Neisseria meningitidis</em>), their mutant forms, as well as Cpf1 from <em>Francisella novicida</em> and <em>Lachnospiraceae/Acadaminoccus</em>. The most intriguing feature of this tool is its ability to add nucleotides in the form of one or two guanines at the 5′-end of the spacer region to improve in vitro transcription. Additionally, two favorable nucleotides adjacent to the PAM region can be considered in order to prevent them from being with the gRNA. This tool exploits the CRISPRater prognosis algorithm to rank predicted gRNAs. gRNAs in intergenic, introns, and exons spacers are presented in various colors. This tool shows information in both table and graphic formats [<a href="#B255-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-ijms-22-05671">255</a>].</p>
<p>The CHOPCHOP tool [<a href="#B264-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B264-ijms-22-05671">264</a>] can be used to design and screen gRNAs with the capability to work in complex with the Cpf1 nuclease and Cas9-nuclease and its nickase forms. The search for gRNA can be run either in all 5′- or 3′-non-coding regions, splicing sites, exons, and in the promoter sequence or in a specified coding region, the length of which would be chosen individually. Moreover, the restriction sites in the edited region can be screened by the corresponding enzymes with the manufacturer’s name. After finishing the search, an adjustable color graphic and an interactive table with ranked gRNA target sequences, consisting of DNA chains, the GC-composition, and other information are provided [<a href="#B255-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B255-ijms-22-05671">255</a>].</p>
<p>The Breaking Cas tool [<a href="#B265-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B265-ijms-22-05671">265</a>] can be applied for designing gRNAs and screening the off-target sites. It is essential to select the form of nuclease from the three types of Cpf1 and the four types of Cas9 with mismatch number and the length of pre-set spacers. It is also possible to select a “Custom” nuclease, which is followed by providing the corresponding lengths of the spacer sequence of gRNA, PAM-sequences, and an acceptable number of mismatches. </p>
<p>All in all, different types of CRISPR-mediated genome editing systems, including targeted mutagenesis to knock out a target gene [<a href="#B266-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B266-ijms-22-05671">266</a>], base editing systems for transition substitution using a combination of Cas9 nickase and either adenosine or cytidine deaminase [<a href="#B267-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B267-ijms-22-05671">267</a>], transversion substitution using Cas9 nickase, cytidine deaminase and uracil DNA glycosylase [<a href="#B268-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B268-ijms-22-05671">268</a>], as well as prime editing systems using DNA or RNA as donors [<a href="#B269-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B269-ijms-22-05671">269</a>], have been recently developed and applied in different species [<a href="#B270-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B270-ijms-22-05671">270</a>]. Very recently the application of the CRISPR system [<a href="#B93-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B93-ijms-22-05671">93</a>] and prediction systems through big data analysis and machine learning [<a href="#B147-ijms-22-05671" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B147-ijms-22-05671">147</a>] have been reported in cannabis. Focusing on cannabis genome engineering, the design of genome sequences is as essential as the applied genome-editing technology. When the target gene is epigenetically regulated, not only genome sequences but also histone modifications, DNA methylation, and chromatin structure, may need to be modified to generate the desired cannabis plant. Furthermore, systems for highly efficient sequence-specific nuclease delivery and subsequent genome-edited cell and plant selection must also be developed to accelerate the breeding of designer cannabis plants (<a href="#ijms-22-05671-f009" class="usa-link">Figure 9</a>).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="ijms-22-05671-f009"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 9.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=8197860_ijms-22-05671-g009.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/9838/8197860/84cb4ef12a67/ijms-22-05671-g009.jpg" loading="lazy" height="820" width="789" alt="Figure 9"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/ijms-22-05671-f009/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>A schematic view of Cannabis genome engineering.</p></figcaption></figure></section></section><section id="sec5-ijms-22-05671"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">5. Conclusions and Future Objectives</h2>
<p>Efficient and reliable in vitro culture procedures can be considered as an important prerequisite for successful gene transformation, genome editing, micropropagation, and conservation of cannabis. Micropropagation is a powerful tool that can develop and propagate new varieties. Polyploidy induction is also a method that can influence secondary metabolites. Although current shoot proliferation-based protocols are relatively developed for mass propagation of cannabis, it is necessary to develop somatic embryogenesis, organogenesis, and haploid production protocols especially for gene transformation and genome editing studies. <em>Agrobacterium</em>-mediated gene transformation has been described in cannabis. Recently, transgenic plant regeneration has been successfully obtained. It seems that using morphogenic genes can help overcome challenges in transgenic plant regeneration. The production of secondary metabolites is another important aspect of in vitro culture of cannabis. Although several studies have attempted to manipulate secondary metabolite production in cell suspension culture and hairy root culture of cannabis, small amounts of cannabinoids have been produced. Therefore, it is necessary to apply new methods such as CRISPR/Cas-equipped <em>A. rhizogenes</em>-mediated hairy root culture for increasing or changing secondary metabolite profiles. Machine learning algorithms can also be considered as robust computational biology for a comprehensive study of secondary metabolites and, also, modeling and optimizing hairy root culture or cell suspension culture for improving its yield. Generally, cannabis plants still provide the most efficient natural sources of secondary metabolites, and, in particular, cannabinoids and modern biotechnologies will play an important role in further genetic improvement. </p></section><section id="notes1"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">Author Contributions</h2>

Springer; Berlin/Heidelberg, Germany: 2015. pp. 165–186.</cite> [<a href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?title=Medicinal%20and%20Aromatic%20Plants%20of%20the%20World&amp;author=S.%20Farag&amp;author=O.%20Kayser&amp;publication_year=2015&amp;" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Google Scholar</a>]</li>

Basis Dis. 2020;1866:165771.  doi: 10.1016/j.bbadis.2020.165771.</cite> [<a href="https://doi.org/10.1016/j.bbadis.2020.165771" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">DOI</a>] [<a href="https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/32201189/" class="usa-link">PubMed</a>] [<a href="https://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?journal=Biochim.%20Biophys.%20Acta%20(Bba)%20Mol.%20Basis%20Dis.&amp;title=The%20molecular%20mechanisms%20that%20underpin%20the%20biological%20benefits%20of%20full-spectrum%20cannabis%20extract%20in%20the%20treatment%20of%20neuropathic%20pain%20and%20inflammation&amp;author=Z.H.%20Maayah&amp;author=S.%20Takahara&amp;author=M.%20Ferdaoussi&amp;author=J.R.B.%20Dyck&amp;volume=1866&amp;publication_year=2020&amp;pages=165771&amp;pmid=32201189&amp;doi=10.1016/j.bbadis.2020.165771&amp;" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Google Scholar</a>]</li>
<li id="B13-ijms-22-05671">
